
---
title: """""""""'Pollen Morphology of Tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae (Caryophyllaceae) and Its Systematic Significance'"""""""""
categories: 
    - 科学期刊
    - BioOne - Featured articles
author: BioOne - Featured articles
comments: false
date: Tue, 27 Oct 2020 00:00:00 GMT
thumbnail: 'https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z3-1_597.jpg'
---

<div>   
<div class="div0" style="margin-bottom:20px;">
                    <div class="row ArticleContentHeadRow"><text class="ArticleContentBoldText">Abstract</text></div>

                                        <div class="row ArticleContentRow">
                        <text class="ArticleContentText"><!-- *** Custom HTML *** --><p class="first" id="ID0EF">Pollen morphology of 79 species, one subspecies and eight varieties representing nine genera of tribe Alsineae and two of tribe Sperguleae was studied using light microscopy (LM) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Among them, 53 species and eight varieties (22 species of which are endemic to China) were reported for the first time. The results demonstrate that the number of pores, the pollen surface ornamentation and the density of microechini have important systematic significance. The surface ornamentation can be divided into three types, namely microechinate-perforate, microechinate-punctate, and microechinate-punctate-perforate. Pollen characteristics support: (i) isolation of <i>Arenaria</i> subgenus <i>Odontostemma</i> from <i>Arenaria</i> and reclassification of <i>Arenaria</i> subgenus <i>Odontostemma</i> to <i>Odontostemma</i>; (ii) isolation of <i>Cerastium</i> subgenus <i>Dichodon</i> from <i>Cerastium</i> and reclassification of <i>Cerastium</i> subgenus <i>Dichodon</i> to <i>Dichodon</i>; (iii) isolation of <i>Minuartia</i> subgenus <i>Rhodalsine</i> from <i>Minuartia</i> and reclassification of <i>Minuartia</i> subgenus <i>Rhodalsine</i> to <i>Rhodalsine</i>; (iv) isolation of <i>Rhodalsine</i> from tribe Alsineae and reclassification of <i>Rhodalsine</i> into tribe Sperguleae; and (v) isolation of <i>Sagina</i> from tribe Alsineae. In addition, the relationships of <i>Arenaria</i> subgenus <i>Eremogone</i> and <i>Arenaria</i> subgenus <i>Eremogoneastrum</i> to tribe Alsineae need to be further studied. Furthermore, pollen characteristics indicate that tribe Alsineae is more evolved than tribe Sperguleae.</p></text>
                    </div>
                </div>
                <div class="div1" style>
                    <div class="row ArticleContentHeadRow"><text class="ArticleContentBoldText"></text></div>

                                        <div class="row ArticleContentRow">
                        <text class="ArticleContentText"><div id="article-body" class="body"><div class="section"><a id="s1" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="main-title">1. Introduction</h2><p id="ID0E3G">The family Caryophyllaceae belongs to the order Caryophyllales (APG IV 2016). It contains about 100 genera and 3000 species, which are mainly distributed in the northern temperate zone with diversified centres located in the Mediterranean and Irano–Turanean region (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr49">Hernández-Ledesma et al. 2015</a>). In traditional taxonomy, according to the types of its stipules, petals, fruits, and embryonic development, Caryophyllaceae is classified into subfamilies Alsinoideae, Caryophylloideae and Paronychioideae. Tribe Alsineae belongs to subfamily Alsinoideae, and tribe Sperguleae belongs to subfamily Paronychioideae (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr09">Bittrich 1993</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr110">Tang et al. 1996</a>).</p><p id="ID0EKH">Different scholars have classified tribe Alsineae differently. <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr85">Pax and Hoffmann (1934)</a> classified tribe Alsineae into subtribe Sabulininae and subtribe Stellariinae according to the way their capsules crack.</p><p id="ID0EQH">McNeill (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr69">1962</a>) divided the tribe Alsineae into three groups: the <i>Arenaria</i> group containing <i>Arenaria</i> L., <i>Brachystemma</i> D.Don, <i>Bufonia</i> L., <i>Gooringia</i> Williams, <i>Honckenya</i> Ehrh., <i>Lepyrodiclis</i> Fenzl, <i>Minuartia</i> L., <i>Moehringia</i> L., <i>Reicheella</i> Pax, <i>Thylacospermum</i> Fenzl, <i>Thurya</i> Boiss. & Balansa and <i>Wilhelmisa</i> Rchb.; the <i>Stellaria-Cerastium</i> group comprising <i>Cerastium</i> L., <i>Myosoton</i> Moench, <i>Holosteum</i> L., <i>Moenchia</i> Ehrh., <i>Pseudostellaria</i> Pax, and <i>Stellaria</i> L.; and the <i>Sagina</i> group containing <i>Sagina</i> L. and <i>Colobanthus</i> Bartl.</p><p id="ID0EFBAC">Bittrich (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr09">1993</a>) believed that according to the presence of nectaries in the stamen and the type of fruit, tribe Alsineae should contain 23 genera, namely <i>Alsinidendron</i> H. Mann, <i>Arenaria</i>, <i>Thylacospermum</i>, <i>Moehringia</i>, <i>Brachystemma</i>, <i>Bufonia</i>, <i>Cerastium</i>, <i>Colobanthus</i>, <i>Holosteum</i>, <i>Honckenya</i>, <i>Lepyrodiclis</i>, <i>Minuartia</i>, <i>Moenchia</i>, <i>Myosoton</i>, <i>Plettkea</i> Mattf., <i>Pycnophyllopsis</i> Skottsb., <i>Pseudostellaria</i>, <i>Reicheella</i>, <i>Sagina</i>, <i>Schiedea</i> Cham. & Schlchtd., <i>Stellaria</i>, <i>Thurya</i> and <i>Wilhelmisa</i>.</p><p id="ID0EZCAC">Tribe Sperguleae includes four genera, namely <i>Rhodalsine</i> J.Gay, <i>Sanctambrosia</i> Skottsb., <i>Spergula</i> L. and <i>Spergularia</i> (Pers.) J.Presl & C.Presl, and about 51 species (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr57">Kool 2012</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr59">Kool and Thulin 2017a</a>). Among them, <i>Rhodalsine</i> has been placed in the tribe Alsineae as <i>Minuartia</i> subgenus <i>Rhodalsine</i> (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr69">McNeill 1962</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr09">Bittrich 1993</a>).</p><p id="ID0EZDAC">Recent phylogenetic studies have proposed to abolish the view that Caryophyllaceae is divided into three subfamilies (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr47">Harbaugh et al. 2010</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr44">Greenberg and Donoghue 2011</a>) and proven that tribe Alsineae is not a monophyletic group and should be re-classified (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr105">Smissen et al. 2002</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr38">Fior et al. 2006</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr39">Fior and Karis 2007</a>). In addition, the infrageneric classification of tribe Alsineae is controversial. For example, the definition of <i>Arenaria</i> has been discrepant (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr36">Fenzl 1840</a>, <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr37">1842</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr08">Bentham and Hooker 1862</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr122">Williams 1895</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr69">McNeill 1962</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr91">Rabeler and Hartman 2005</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr95">Sadeghian et al. 2014</a>, <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr96">2015</a>). The intrageneric classification of <i>Cerastium</i> has also drawn significant attention (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr100">Schischkin 1970</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr21">Czerepanov 1995</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr99">Scheen et al. 2004</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr04">Arabi et al. 2017</a>, <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr05">2018</a>). <i>Minuartia</i> has been proven to not be a monophyletic group, and its infrageneric classification is inconclusive (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr63">Löve and Löve 1975</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr27">Dillenberger and Kadereit 2014</a>).</p><p id="ID0ERGAC">The species origin, taxonomy and phylogenetic relationships of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae have been studied from the aspects of seed morphology (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr31">Echlin 1968</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr124">Wofford 1981</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr127">Wyatt 1984</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr74">Minuto et al. 2006</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr15">Casazza and Minuto 2008</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr73">Memon et al. 2010</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr75">Minuto et al. 2011</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr79">Mostafavi et al. 2011</a>, <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr80">2013</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr54">Kanwal et al. 2012</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr56">Koç et al. 2012</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr95">Sadeghian et al. 2014</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr62">Lorite et al. 2018</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr115">Ullah, Zafar, Amhad, Sultana et al. 2018</a>), plant external morphology (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr12">Brysting and Elven 2000</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr19">Conti 2003</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr55">Koç et al. 2019</a>), anatomy (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr101">Schweingruber 2007</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr82">Mousavi et al. 2019</a>), cell biology (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr10">Boşcaiu et al. 1999</a>), biogeography (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr77">Moore et al. 2013</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr51">Iamonico 2015</a>) and molecular biology (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr13">Brysting et al. 2011</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr77">Moore and Kadereit 2013</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr28">Dillenberger and Kadereit 2017</a>).</p><p id="ID0EXJAC">Pollen is the male gametophyte of plants. Compared with other morphological characteristics such as roots, stems, leaves, etc., it has high genetic stability. The component sporopollenin of its exine is resistant to acid and alkali treatments (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr33">Erdtman 1969</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr121">Wang et al. 1997</a>). The morphological characteristics of pollen grains are not only widely used in the fields of palaeobotany, palaeoecology and atmospheric palynology, but also play important roles in revealing species origin, phylogenetic relationships and plant taxonomy (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr118">Walker and Doyle 1975</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr97">Sahreen et al. 2008</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr112">Tuler et al. 2017</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr107">Song et al. 2018</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr45">Gul et al. 2019</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr113">Ullah, Zaman et al. 2019</a>).</p><p id="ID0EZKAC">Pollen morphology (such as number of pores, density of microechini, surface ornamentation and exine thickness) of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae has been widely used in taxonomy at various levels, including intergeneric and intrageneric, interspecies and intraspecies classifications (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr16">Chanda 1962</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr70">McNeill and Basset 1974</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr109">Taia 1994</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr130">Yıldız 2001</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr86">Perveen and Qaiser 2006</a>).</p><p id="ID0EPLAC">Although many researchers have studied the pollen grains of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae, they only focused on description of pollen morphological features of a few species and genera, and some studies only described some of the pollen characteristics (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr34">Erdtman et al. 1961</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr32">Erdtman 1968</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr83">Nowicke 1975</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr103">Skvarla and Nowicke 1976</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr52">Iwarsson 1977</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr03">Al-Eisawi 1989</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr26">Diaz de la Guardia et al. 1991</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr84">Parent and Richard 1993</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr89">Punt and Hoen 1995</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr25">Dang et al. 1998</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr17">Chen et al. 2009</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr11">Bozchaloyi and Keshavarz 2014</a>).</p><p id="ID0EBNAC">Candau (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr14">1978</a>) studied the diameter, shape and surface ornamentation of pollen grains, as well as the aperture diameter of 37 species of nine genera of tribe Alsineae in subfamily Alsinoideae and classified the pollen surface ornamentation of tribe Alsineae as microechinate and microperforate. But he did not describe the aperture spacing, the number of granules on the operculum, or the density of microechini.</p><p id="ID0EHNAC"><a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr23">Dang and Jiang (1995)</a> studied the pollen morphology of 12 species of three genera of the tribe Alsineae in China and found that their pollen grains are spheroidal or nearly spherical and have pantoporate apertures with warty granules, a protruding or depressed membrane, and granulate surface ornamentation with small grooves.</p><p id="ID0EMNAC">Dang and Jiang (1996) studied the pollen morphology of two species of <i>Spergularia</i> in China, and believed that their pollen grains are oblong in the polar view and spherical in the equatorial view, with a tricolpate aperture and granulate surface ornamentation.</p><p id="ID0EQNAC"><a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr86">Perveen and Qaiser (2006)</a> studied the pollen morphology of 74 species of 23 genera of Caryophyllaceae in Turkey, including 19 species of six genera in tribe Alsineae, and five species of two genera in tribe Sperguleae. They described the pollen size and shape, and divided the pollen surface ornamentation into spinulose-punctate and scabrate-punctuate types. However, they did not describe the spacing and number of apertures or the number of granules on the operculum, nor did they discuss the relevant taxonomic issues.</p><p id="ID0EVNAC"><a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr81">Mostafavi and Mehregan (2014)</a> studied the pollen of 20 species of <i>Minuartia</i> in Iran and found that their pollen grains are nearly spherical or polyhedral, and have microechinate and microechinate-punctate surface ornamentations.</p><p id="ID0E3NAC"><a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr120">Wan and Liu (2017)</a> studied the pollen of 64 species and five varieties of <i>Stellaria</i> in China and found that their pollen grains are spherical or spherical-polyhedral, with pantoporate apertures and microechinate-perforate, microechinate-punctate and microechinate-punctate-perforate surface ornamentations.</p><p id="ID0EDOAC"><a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr29">Doğan and Ömer Erdem (2018)</a> studied the pollen of 11 taxa belonging to <i>Arenaria</i> and found that their pollen grains are oblong with pantoporate apertures and microechinate-perforate surface ornamentation.</p><p id="ID0EKOAC">Ullah, Shah et al. (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr113">2019</a>) described the pollen of 18 species of seven genera of subfamily Alsinoideae from Pakistan and found that their pollen grains with echini on the surface are subspheroidal or prolate and have microechinate-punctate or microechinate-perforate surface ornamentation.</p><p id="ID0EQOAC">In this study, the pollen morphology of nine genera including 77 species, one subspecies and eight varieties of the tribe Alsineae and two genera including two species of the tribe Sperguleae are observed and studied in detail using light microscopy (LM) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Among them, 45 species, one subspecies and five varieties are found in China and 22 species are endemic to China. In addition, the pollen morphology of 53 species and eight varieties is reported for the first time. This study aims to provide palynological information for the taxonomic and phylogenetic study of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae.</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s2" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="main-title">2. Materials and methods</h2><div class="section"><a id="s2a" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">2.1. Experimental materials</h3><p id="ID0EXOAC">Pollen grains of the studied species were collected from the herbarium of the South China Botanical Garden, Chinese Academy of Sciences (IBSC) and the herbarium of the Institute of Applied Ecology, Chinese Academy of Sciences (IFP). The voucher information for all specimens is given in Table S1 (in the supplementary materials). In addition, the species were confirmed on the basis of characters mentioned in the original descriptions of Flora of China and The Plant List. Their names are accepted in Tropicos and the International Plant Names Index (IPNI). The geographical distribution of the samples is marked on the map presented in <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#f01">Figure 1</a>.</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="f01" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Figure 1.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0EDPAC">Occurrence map of the analysed species of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae, showing the distribution of sample locations in (A) Asia and Europe, (B) America and (C) New Guinea. Map data © 2019 Google Maps. Scale bar: 400 km.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z3-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z3-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z3-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s2b" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">2.2. Methods</h3><p id="ID0EJPAC">The naturally dried pollen grains were prepared for LM by acetolysis (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr33">Erdtman 1969</a>), then mounted in glycerin jelly. All measurements were taken within 7days of preparation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr71">Melhem and Matos 1972</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr98">Salgado-Labouriau 1973</a>). Images were captured using a Leica DM6000 upright fluorescence microscope.</p><p id="ID0EXPAC">For the SEM study, pollen grains were not subjected to acetolysis, to preserve the exine and intine (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr48">Harley and Ferguson 1990</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr46">Halbritter and Hesse 2004</a>). The non-acetolysed pollen grains were scattered evenly on aluminium stubs covered with double-sided transparent adhesive tape. All samples were coated with gold-palladium, examined under a Hitachi S-4800 scanning electron microscope at 10kV and photographed.</p><p id="ID0ECAAE">Twenty-five pollen grains of each species were randomly selected, and their diameter, the diameter of the pores or the colpus length and width, the distance between two pores, the exine thickness and the density of microechini (the number of microechini in a 5 µm × 5 µm area of pollen grain surface) were measured. Statistical analyses were conducted to obtain arithmetic means, standard deviations and 95% confidence interval (CI 95%). To compare the values of the pollen grain diameter, we used the graphs of SPSS version 22.0 (IBM SPSS Statistics), which represent the mean and the confidence interval values.</p><p id="ID0EDAAE">Palynological terminology is used from previous works (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr33">Erdtman 1969</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr121">Wang et al. 1997</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr90">Punt et al. 2007</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr50">Hesse et al. 2009</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s3" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="main-title">3. Results</h2><p id="ID0EYAAE">The detailed pollen characteristics of all examined samples are listed in Tables S2–S4 (supplementary materials) and <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p01">Plates 1</a>–<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">17</a>. The pollen characteristics of each genus are described as follows.</p><div class="section"><a id="s3a" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.1. Arenaria</h3><p id="ID0EFBAE"><i>Arenaria</i> contains about 300 species worldwide. Among them, 102 species are found in China, 79 of which are endemic to China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr126">Wu et al. 2001</a>). In this study, 30 species and one variety are investigated, of which 26 species and one variety are reported for the first time.</p><p id="ID0EMBAE"><i>Arenaria</i> has spherical or spheroidal-polyhedral pollen grains with a diameter of 20.76–40.10µm, and has 10–18 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 2.89–5.90µm and the distance between two pores is 4.56–9.41µm. There are 5–17 granules on the operculum. Exine thickness is 1.15–3.85µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5µm × 5µm ranges from 6 to 15. The pollen grains have microechinate-perforate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p10">Plate 10</a>, figures 15–16) microechinate-punctate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p09">Plate 9</a>, figures 17–18) and microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p10">Plate 10</a>, figures 5–6).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p01" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 1.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0EBCAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Arenaria arundana</i>; 3–4. <i>A. atuntziensis</i>; 5–6. <i>A. barbata</i>; 7–8. <i>A. brevipetala</i>; 9–10. <i>A. ciliata</i>; 11–12. <i>A. debilis</i>; 13–14. <i>A. delavayi</i>; 15–16. <i>A. dimorphitricha</i>; 17–18. <i>A. erinacea</i>; 19–20. <i>A. euodonta</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z4-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z4-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z4-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s3b" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.2. Bufonia</h3><p id="ID0E3CAE"><i>Bufonia</i> includes 20 species worldwide (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr09">Bittrich 1993</a>). In this research, we studied the species <i>Bufonia parviflora</i> Grisb., and its pollen morphology is reported for the first time.</p><p id="ID0EFDAE">The pollen grains of <i>Bufonia</i> are spheroidal-polyhedral with a diameter of 15.65–18.61 µm, and have 10–12 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 2.18–3.57 µm and the distance between two pores is 3.17–5.75 µm. There are 5–10 granules on the operculum. Exine thickness is 1.21–2.41 µm. The number of microechini in a sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 11 to 16. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p12">Plate 12</a>, figures 3–4).</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s3c" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.3. Cerastium</h3><p id="ID0EQDAE"><i>Cerastium</i> comprises approximately 100 species worldwide. Among them, 29 species are found in China, nine of which are endemic to China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>). In this study, 26 species, one subspecies and five varieties are investigated, of which 17 species and five varieties are reported for the first time.</p><p id="ID0EXDAE">The pollen grains of <i>Cerastium</i> are spherical or spheroidal-polyhedral with a diameter of 20.67–35.81 µm, and have 10–20 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 2.36–5.73 µm and the distance between two pores is 4.54–9.50 µm. There are 6–13 granules on the operculum. The exine thickness is 1.12–3.05 µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 7 to 16. The pollen grains have microechinate-perforate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p14">Plate 14</a>, figures 1–2), microechinate-punctate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p13">Plate 13</a>, figures 13–14) and microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p14">Plate 14</a>, figures 15–16).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p02" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 2.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0ENEAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Arenaria fimbriata</i>; 3–4. <i>A. foliosa</i>; 5–6. <i>A. grandiflora</i>; 7–8. <i>A. ionandra</i>; 9–10. <i>A. juncea</i> var<i>. glabra</i>; 11–12. <i>A. leucasteria</i>; 13–14. <i>A. longifolia</i>; 15–16. <i>A. longipetiolata</i>; 17–18. <i>A. longistyla</i>; 19–20. <i>A. napuligera</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z5-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z5-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z5-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s3d" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.4. Holosteum</h3><p id="ID0EKFAE"><i>Holosteum</i> has about four species worldwide, but only one species in China, namely <i>Holosteum umbellatum</i> L. (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>).</p><p id="ID0ETFAE">The pollen grains of <i>Holosteum</i> are spheroidal-polyhedral with a diameter of 24.55–31.29 µm, and have 16–20 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 1.98–4.36 µm, and the distance between two pores is 3.56–7.33 µm. There are 8–17 granules on the operculum. The exine thickness is 1.38–2.59 µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 13 to 18. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p15">Plate 15</a>, figures 9–10).</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s3e" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.5. Lepyrodiclis</h3><p id="ID0E5FAE"><i>Lepyrodiclis</i> has about three species worldwide, of which two species are found in China. Here we describe one species, namely <i>Lepyrodiclis holosteoides</i> (C. A. Mey.) Fisch. et Mey. (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p03" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 3.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0ENGAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Arenaria orbiculata</i>; 3–4. <i>A. oreophila</i>; 5–6. <i>A. pseudostellaria</i>; 7–8. <i>A. quadridentata</i>; 9–10. <i>A. serpyllifolia</i>; 11–12. <i>A. stenophylla</i>; 13–14. <i>A. xerophila</i>; 15–16. <i>A. yunnanensis</i>; 17–18. <i>Bufonia parviflora</i>; 19–20. <i>Cerastium alpinum</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z6-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z6-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z6-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div><p id="ID0EFHAE">The pollen grains of <i>Lepyrodiclis</i> are spheroidal with a diameter of 25.15–30.89 µm, and have 28–32 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 1.58–2.77 µm and the distance between two pores is 2.97–5.54 µm. There are 3–8 granules on the operculum. The exine thickness is 1.21–2.59 µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 16 to 22. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p15">Plate 15</a>, figures 11–12).</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s3f" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.6. Minuartia</h3><p id="ID0EQHAE"><i>Minuartia</i> contains about 120 species worldwide. Among them, nine species are found in China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>). In this study, 12 species and two varieties are investigated, of which six species and two varieties are reported for the first time.</p><p id="ID0EXHAE">The pollen grains of <i>Minuartia</i> (excluding subgenus <i>Rhodalsine</i>) are spheroidal with a diameter of 19.56–29.90µm, and have 12–20 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 1.94–4.35µm, and the distance between two pores is 4.01–8.14µm. There are 6–12 granules on the operculum. The exine thickness is 1.07–3.94µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5µm × 5µm ranges from 12 to 20. The pollen grains have microechinate-perforate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p16">Plate 16</a>, figures 17–18), microechinate-punctate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p16">Plate 16</a>, figures 5–6) and microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p15">Plate 15</a>, figures 15–16).</p><p id="ID0EJIAE">The pollen morphology of subgenus <i>Rhodalsine</i> is special. Pollen grains of the subgenus <i>Rhodalsine</i> are small in size. The average length of the polar axis (P) is 13.19 (11.96–15.14) µm and the average length of the (E) equatorial axis is 10.72 (7.84–12.85) µm. The P/E ratio is 1.25, the grains being subprolate in shape. They are subcircular in equatorial view and semi-triangular in polar view, and tricolpate. The surface of the colpus membrane is granulate. The exine thickness is 2.58 (1.72–3.53) µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 75 to 125. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p15">Plate 15</a>, figures 19–20; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p16">Plate 16</a>, figures 1–2).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p04" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 4.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0E4IAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Cerastium alsinifolium</i>; 3–4. <i>C. anomalum</i>; 5–6. <i>C. arvense</i>; 7–8. <i>C. baischanense</i>; 9–10. <i>C. cerastoides</i>; 11–12. <i>C. dahuricum</i>; 13–14. <i>C. fischerianum</i>; 15–16. <i>C. fontanum</i>; 17–18. <i>C. fontanum</i> subsp<i>. vulgare</i>; 19–20. <i>C. glomeratum</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z7-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z7-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z7-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s3g" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.7. Moehringia</h3><p id="ID0E1JAE"><i>Moehringia</i> has about 25 species worldwide, of which three species are found in China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>). Here we describe one species, namely <i>Moehringia trinervia</i> (L.) Clairv.</p><p id="ID0EDKAE">The pollen grains of <i>Moehringia</i> are spheroidal with a diameter of 20.96–25.61 µm, and have 10–14 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 2.62–3.69 µm and the distance between two pores is 4.82–7.18 µm. There are 5–10 granules on the operculum. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 18 to 21. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 3–4).</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s3h" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.8. Sagina</h3><p id="ID0EOKAE"><i>Sagina</i> contains about 30 species worldwide. Among them, four species are found in China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>). In this study, four species are investigated, of which two species are reported for the first time.</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p05" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 5.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0E2KAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Cerastium glutinosum</i>; 3–4. <i>C. holosteoides</i> var<i>. angustifolium</i>; 5–6. <i>C. limprichtii</i>; 7–8. <i>C. lithospermifolium</i>; 9–10. <i>C. maximum</i>; 11–12. <i>C. nutans</i>; 13–14. <i>C. oblongifolium</i>; 15–16. <i>C. pauciflorum</i> var. <i>oxalidiflorum</i>; 17–18. <i>C. polymorphum</i>; 19–20. <i>C. ponticum</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z8-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z8-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z8-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div><p id="ID0EXLAE">The pollen grains of <i>Sagina</i> are spheroidal or spheroidal-polyhedral with a diameter of 22.80–24.73 µm, and have 24–36 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 1.13–1.66 µm and the distance between two pores is 2.90–4.77 µm. There are 2–5 granules on the operculum. The exine thickness is 0.43–1.77 µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 9 to 14. The pollen grains have microechinate-perforate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 9–10), microechinate-punctate (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 11–12) and microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 7–8).</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s3i" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.9. Schiedea</h3><p id="ID0EKMAE"><i>Schiedea</i> consists of 34 species worldwide (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr09">Bittrich 1993</a>). In this research, we studied the species <i>Schiedea ligustrina</i> Cham. & Schlecht., and its pollen morphology is reported for the first time.</p><p id="ID0ETMAE">The pollen grains of <i>Schiedea</i> are spheroidal-polyhedral with a diameter of 17.14–20.56 µm, and have 14–18 round pores uniformly distributed on the surface. The diameter of the pores is 1.13–2.11 µm and the distance between two pores is 2.82–4.79 µm. There are 4–7 granules on the operculum. The exine thickness is 1.72–2.76 µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5 µm × 5 µm ranges from 12 to 20. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 13–14).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p06" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 6.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0EBNAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Cerastium rubescens</i> var. <i>ovatum</i>; 3–4. <i>C. silvaticum</i>; 5–6. <i>C. takasagomontanum</i>; 7–8. <i>C. tianschanicum</i>; 9–10. <i>C. tomentosum</i>; 11–12. <i>C. trigynum</i> var<i>. morrisonen</i>; 13–14. <i>C. wilsoni</i>; 15–16. <i>Holosteum umbellatum</i>; 17–18. <i>Lepyrodiclis holosteoides</i>; 19–20. <i>Minuartia abchasica</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z9-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z9-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z9-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s3j" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.10. Spergula</h3><p id="ID0EAOAE"><i>Spergula</i> consists of five species worldwide, with only one species in China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>). In this research, we studied the species <i>Spergula arvensis</i> L.</p><p id="ID0EJOAE">Pollen grains of <i>Spergula</i> are small in size. The average length of the polar axis is 18.06 (16.05–20.08) µm, and the average length of the equatorial axis is 15.87 (13.92–17.30) µm. The P/E ratio is 1.14, the grains being subprolate in shape. They are subcircular in equatorial view and semi-triangular in polar view, and tricolpate. The surface of the colpus membrane is granulate. The exine thickness is 0.75 (0.50–1.10) µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5µm × 5µm ranges from 63 to 100. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 15–18).</p></div><div class="section"><a id="s3k" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.11. Spergularia</h3><p id="ID0EUOAE"><i>Spergularia</i> consists of 25 species worldwide, but there are only four species in China (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr64">Lu et al. 2001</a>). We studied the species <i>Spergularia marina</i> (L.) Griseb.</p><p id="ID0E4OAE">Pollen grains of <i>Spergularia</i> are small in size. The average length of the polar axis is 28.40 (25.64–38.08) µm, and the average length of the equatorial axis is 16.95 (13.08–19.91) µm. The P/E ratio is 1.70, the grains being prolate in shape. They are subcircular in equatorial view and broad-elliptic in polar view, and tricolpate. The surface of the colpus membrane is microechinate. The exine thickness is 0.87 (0.64–1.39) µm. The number of microechini per sample area of 5µm × 5µm ranges from 65 to 100. The pollen grains have microechinate-punctate-perforate ornamentation (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p17">Plate 17</a>, figures 18–20).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p07" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 7.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0ELPAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribe Alsineae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Minuartia arctica</i> var<i>. hondoensis</i>; 3–4. <i>M. fasciculata</i>; 5–6. <i>M. geniculata</i>; 7–8. <i>M. hybrida</i>; 9–10. <i>M. kashmirica</i>; 11–12. <i>M. kitaibelii</i>; 13–14. <i>M. kryloviana</i>; 15–16. <i>M. laricifolia</i>; 17–18. <i>M. laricina</i>; 19–20. <i>M. lineata</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z10-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z10-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z10-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s3l" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">3.12. Statistical analysis</h3><p id="ID0EIQAE">For quantitative analysis of the pollen grain size (excluding tricolpate pollen), we found that <i>Bufonia parviflora</i> is separated from the other species by a lower pollen diameter. <i>Arenaria trichophora</i> Franch. is separated from other species by a higher pollen diameter. The other species cannot be separated by pollen size because of the overlapping ranges of their pollen diameters (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#f02">Figure 2</a>).</p></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s4" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="main-title">4. Discussion</h2><div class="section"><a id="s4a" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h3 class="section-title">4.1. Pollen morphology</h3><div class="section"><a id="s4a1" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h4 class="subsection-title">4.1.1. Pollen size</h4><p id="ID0E2QAE">Our study revealed that the pollen of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae was small (10–25µm) or medium (26–50µm) in size, consistent with the results of previous studies (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr52">Iwarsson 1977</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr03">Al-Eisawi 1989</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr81">Mostafavi and Mehregan 2014</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr29">Doğan and Ömer Erdem 2018</a>). However, pollen size is affected by fixation media (such as glycerin gel, silicone oil, etc.), chemical treatment methods (acid treatment vs potassium hydroxide treatment, dehydration vs non-dehydration, etc.) and some ecological factors (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr89">Punt and Hoen 1995</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr111">Torres 2000</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr72">Meltsov et al. 2008</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr07">Ataşlar et al. 2009</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr125">Wronska-Pilarek et al. 2010</a>). Some scholars have suggested that pollen size is not meaningful in the taxonomy of Caryophyllaceae (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr120">Wan and Liu 2017</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr20">Cui et al. 2018</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr119">Wan et al. 2018</a>). Our quantitative analysis showed that the pollen size of different species overlapped and thus could not be used to distinguish them (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#f02">Figure 2</a>).</p><div class="fig panel" style="display: float; clear: both"><a id="p08" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h2 class="label">Plate 8.</h2><div class="caption"><p class="first" id="ID0EXSAE">Light microscopy (LM) micrographs of tribes Alsineae and Sperguleae pollen grains. 1–2. <i>Minuartia macrocarpa</i> var<i>. koreana</i>; 3–4. <i>M. meyeri</i>; 5–6. <i>M. verna</i>; 7–8. <i>Sagina japonica</i>; 9–10. <i>S. maxima</i>; 11–12. <i>S. papuana</i>; 13–14. <i>S. saginoides</i>; 15–16. <i>Schiedea ligustrina</i>; 17–18. <i>Spergula arvensis</i>; 19–20. <i>Spergularia marina</i>. Scale bar: 10 µm.</p></div><a target="_blank" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/img-z11-1_597.jpg"><img alt="img-z11-1_597.jpg" src="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/ContentImages/Journals/paly/44/4/01916122.2019.1661882/graphic/WebImages/img-z11-1_597.jpg" referrerpolicy="no-referrer"></a></div></div><div class="section"><a id="s4a2" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/undefined"><!-- named anchor --></a><h4 class="subsection-title">4.1.2. Pollen shape</h4><p id="ID0EUTAE">Our results show that the pollen grains of tribe Alsineae are spheroidal or spheroidal-polyhedral and the pollen grains of tribe Sperguleae are subprolate or prolate, consistent with previous results (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr14">Candau 1978</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr86">Perveen and Qaiser 2006</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr17">Chen et al. 2009</a>). However, some studies have shown that pollen shape is susceptible to treatment methods (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr76">Moon et al. 2008</a>; <a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#bibr40">Fourny et al. 2018</a>). In this study, the shape of pollen grains changed after acid treatment compared to that without treatment. For example, pollen grains of <i>Arenaria debilis</i> are spheroidal without acid treatment (<a class="internal-link" href="https://bioone.org/journals/palynology/volume-44/issue-4/01916122.2019.1661882/Pollen-Morphology-of-Tribes-Alsineae-and-Sperguleae-Caryophyllaceae-and-Its/10.1080/01916122.2019.1661882.full#p01">Plate 1</a>, figures 11–12).
